Jobless Claims Hit Lowest Level Since 2008
By Alan Zibel and Jeff Bater
595 words
17 February 2012
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

The number of workers filing new applications for unemployment benefits fell last week to the lowest level in nearly four years, one of a series of reports offering mostly positive signs about the U.S. economy.

Initial unemployment claims dropped by 13,000 to 348,000 in the week ended Feb. 11, the Labor Department said Thursday. The decline in the weekly report was the third in a row and carried new jobless claims to their lowest level since early March 2008, before the financial crisis broke out that year.

Separately, home building rose slightly in January, but not enough to signal that the housing industry is ready to recover from the worst downturn in decades. Meanwhile, wholesale prices rose only modestly in January, as energy and food costs declined, but underlying prices jumped by a larger amount.

"The housing starts and unemployment claims numbers add to the belief that the economy is shifting gears," said economist Joel Naroff, of Naroff Economic Advisors.

The positive reports are likely to fuel a continuing debate among Federal Reserve policy makers about whether to engage in a new round of bond-buying to aid an economy that is growing only modestly more than two years after the end of a deep recession.

Minutes released Wednesday of the central bank's January meeting showed officials appeared divided over more bond purchases, which would seek to spur investment by pushing down long-term interest rates.

This month, the government reported nonfarm employment rose by 243,000 jobs in January, the biggest gain in nine months. as the unemployment rate fell to 8.3%.

Thursday's report on the prices producers pay for goods and services showed an overall increase of only 0.1% in January. But core inflation, which strips out volatile food and energy, rose a heftier 0.4%.

The jump in core inflation could reflect start-of-the-year increases in prices of drugs and other goods rather than an accelerating trend, said Nigel Gault, chief U.S. economist at IHS Global Insight.

January housing starts rose 1.5% from December to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 699,000, up almost 10% from a year earlier, the Commerce Department said. That figure was helped by an 8.5% increase in multifamily-home starts, a volatile part of the market.

Construction of single-family homes, which made up about 73% of housing starts, fell by 1% on a monthly basis, though it was still up by more than 16% from the same month a year earlier.

"Home builders are shaking off the shackles of the last five years and are beginning to contribute to [economic] growth," said Paul Dales, an economist with Capital Economics. "That said, the housing sector is currently not big enough to set the economy alight."

In an encouraging sign, the share of homeowners behind on their mortgage hit the lowest level in three years in the fourth quarter of 2011, a report said Thursday. But the share of loans in foreclosure remained near the highest levels of the housing crisis, meaning the sector could face continued pressure as banks take over and resell homes.

The survey by the Mortgage Bankers Association showed 7.6% of residential mortgages were at least 30 days past due at the end of 2011, against 8.3% a year ago.

Abreast of the Market
U.S. Stocks Advance on Upbeat Data --- Positive News on Housing Starts, Jobless Claims; GM Jumps on Strong Profit; In Europe, 'Fat Finger' Error?
By Tomi Kilgore and Chris Dieterich
633 words
17 February 2012
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
26
English
(c) 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

NEW YORK -- U.S. stocks rose Thursday as positive news from the labor and housing markets deflected concern about Greece's debt.

The Dow Jones Industrial Average was up 110.65 points, or 0.9%, to 12891.60 in midday trade. Blue chips rebounded after falling 97 points on Wednesday, the Dow's worst session of the year. The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index rose 0.9% to 1354.79, and the Nasdaq Composite climbed 1% to 2944.52.

All 10 sectors in the S&P 500 rose, as utility and financial stocks led the advance.

Stocks rose early after a full slate of U.S. economic data encouraged investors.

The number of U.S. workers applying for unemployment benefits fell last week to the lowest level in nearly four years. Initial claims dropped by 13,000 to 348,000 last week. Economists surveyed by Dow Jones Newswires expected claims to rise by 7,000, to a total of 365,000.

Housing starts increased to an adjusted annual rate of 699,000 last month, while economists had expected starts to total 677,000. Issuance of building permits for housing rose 0.7% in January, in line with expectations.

"The trend in jobless claims has been encouraging, and on the whole the data has been firmer in the recent months," said Josh Feinman, global chief economist for DB Advisors in New York.

Wholesale prices rose modestly in January. The producer-price index climbed a seasonally adjusted 0.1%, shy of the expected 0.4% rise. But core prices, which exclude energy and food, rose 0.4% versus an expected 0.2% rise.

The Philadelphia Fed reported that an index of business conditions for manufacturers in the mid-Atlantic region rose to 10.2 in February from 7.3 in January, slightly better than expectations for a reading of 10.0.

Downgrade fears fanned more euro-zone worries. Moody's Investors Service placed the ratings of more than 100 European financial institutions and 16 European countries on review for possible downgrade.

In corporate news, General Motors rose 6% after the auto maker reported a record profit of $7.6 billion for 2011, but also losses in Europe and thin profit margins in the fourth quarter.

Amazon.com fell 4.3% after analysts at Morgan Stanley trimmed the stock's rating to "equalweight" from "overweight," warning that Apple's iPhones and iPads sales could take away market share in electronic books. The stock fell even after a report from IHS Inc. showed Amazon's Kindle fire and other low-priced tablets cut into Apple's market share in the fourth quarter.

European Stocks

Shares declined as investors digested conflicting information about whether Greece will get its 130 billion euros loan tranche before its March 20 bond redemption payment becomes due. Moody's warning about possible bank downgrades also hit sentiment.

The U.K.'s FTSE 100 index closed 0.1% lower at 5885.38. Germany's DAX fell 0.1% to 6751.96 and France's CAC-40 closed 0.1% higher at 3393.25.

The Stoxx Europe 600 index slumped heavily in the last minute of trading to close down 10% at 236.90, but traders attributed this fall to a technical glitch or what is known as a "fat finger" or input error. Index specialist Stoxx Limited was unavailable to confirm this. The blue-chip Euro Stoxx 50 index ended down 0.2% at 2489.35.

Banks suffered the brunt of the selling.

Heard on the Street
Surge in Chinese Stocks Looks Vulnerable
By Tom Orlik
396 words
16 February 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C12
English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

[Financial Analysis and Commentary]

The party for China's equities might be over before it even has begun.

The Shanghai market has risen 7.6% so far in 2012, after a 22% drop during 2011. The surge was supported by bullish assumptions about the direction of policy on bank lending and home sales. Now that those assumptions are starting to come unstuck, the market's recent run looks unsustainable.

On property, investors were cheered last Thursday when Wuhu, a town in eastern China, broke ranks from the two-year national clampdown on house prices and announced subsidies for home buyers. But on Saturday, with Beijing smarting, Wuhu made an abrupt about-face. The central case remains one where controls on the property sector remain.

That is bad news for equities. Slower property sales will dent earnings at developers, steel and cement firms that populate the mainland index. Flat or falling house prices might encourage investors to favor equities. But analysts at CLSA say any substitution effect is less powerful than the negative wealth effect.

Expectations that more ample credit would find its way into the stock markets also have faded. New loans for January came in at a pitiful 738.1 billion yuan ($117.2 billion), down 22.9% from a year earlier. Reports in the official media suggest February's new loans also are subdued -- further evidence the new loan quota for the year will be below the 8 trillion to 8.5 trillion yuan markets had hoped for.

The rapid growth of wealth-management products also will dent demand for equities. In the past, investors wanting to beat inflation had little choice but to risk a ride on the stock-market roller coaster. Wealth-management products provide another option, offering higher returns than bank deposits without the palpitations of equities. China's wealth-management products typically invest in money markets, or high-interest loans, not stocks.

As demand for equities falls, supply might be set to increase. Ernst & Young says initial public offerings on the mainland could reach 300 billion yuan in 2012, up about 10% from 2011. With reduced demand chasing increased supply, the minirally in China's equity markets in the first weeks of the year looks difficult to sustain.

U.S. News: New Tower Would Fill Boston's Scar
By Eliot Brown
949 words
13 February 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
A3
English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

BOSTON -- This city is finally preparing to heal a hole in its heart: the site of the former Filene's flagship department store that for more than three years has been a stalled real-estate development in the center of downtown.

A New York developer, Millennium Partners, has agreed to buy a controlling stake in the long-delayed project with new plans for developing a mixed-use tower, the city announced earlier this month. If finalized, the deal would bring to an end a high-profile war waged by Boston Mayor Thomas Menino against Vornado Realty Trust, which has controlled the site for years.

A five-term mayor, Mr. Menino tried for two years to publicly bully Vornado into restarting its planned project, a 39-story hotel-office-apartment tower that was halted in the middle of excavation as the economy soured. He called the developer arrogant, threatened to use eminent domain and revoked its permits to build.

"It's my fiduciary responsibility to make sure a property like that gets redeveloped as quickly as possible," Mr. Menino said in an interview at City Hall. "You're not going to hold up the city of Boston."

Mr. Menino said he met recently with Vornado, one of the country's largest owners of office and retail property, and they agreed the company and city would start a new chapter. Vornado had previously said the drop in property values and the economy meant it couldn't restart the project.

Under the Millennium agreement, Vornado would keep a noncontrolling stake in the project, according to the mayor's office.

Millennium declined to go into detail about its plans, saying in an emailed statement that it "will design and construct a building worthy of the site's significance and prominence."

Mr. Menino's aggressive approach comes at a time when similar stalled sites dot cities across the U.S., a vestige of the real-estate boom turned bust.

New Jersey Gov. Chris Christie has pledged tax abatements to developers in an attempt to complete the Revel casino in Atlantic City and a stalled $1.8 billion retail and entertainment center previously named Xanadu in the Meadowlands outside Manhattan.

In New York, an array of government agencies in 2010 pledged hundreds of millions of dollars to aid completion of one to two privately owned office towers at the World Trade Center site, depending on how much space is leased.

Other cities, meanwhile, have taken a more laissez faire approach such as Chicago, where prominent development sites have remained boarded up, including a giant circular foundation along Lake Shore Drive that was to house what would have been the tallest tower in the U.S. at 2,000 feet.

In Boston, frustrations over the halted Vornado project resonated throughout the city. Ire over the pit has been the subject of numerous editorials in both of Boston's daily papers. The stalled site was the main focus of an environmental design class at Northeastern University last fall, and earlier this year, the City Council president, Stephen Murphy, sought to block an unrelated casino project in which Vornado was an investor until the company made progress downtown.

Such frustrations speak to the nostalgia some Bostonians have for their compact downtown and its rich history. The pit was the site of part of the flagship Filene's Department Store and Filene's Basement, a discount-shopping institution in the center of a once-vibrant shopping district called Downtown Crossing. Both Filene's and Filene's Basement have gone out of business.

The exterior of the oldest portion of Filene's still stands, protected by a historic-preservation designation but awaiting a new use. A Macy's operates across the street in what for decades was Jordan Marsh, Filene's arch rival, while numerous smaller stores draw shoppers past the hole in the ground.

The area declined through the past half-century, and in recent years, the Menino administration made its revitalization a priority. The planned office, condo and retail project was to be the centerpiece. Instead, after Vornado stopped construction in 2008, the pit left more blight.

"It was a key part of the rejuvenation of the area," said Jerold Kayden, an urban planning professor at Harvard University. "To have a hole there is devastating."

To be sure, the start of new construction is by no means a fait accompli. Millennium hasn't yet completed its deal, which involves acquiring the stakes of Vornado's current partners -- a fund managed by J.P. Morgan Chase & Co., Mack-Cali Realty Corp. and Boston Global Investors -- and putting in an unspecified amount of new money, according to the Boston Redevelopment Authority.

It's a tough time to get construction financing for any project, one of the reasons it has taken so long for Vornado to bring in a new partner.

The redevelopment authority said it believes Millennium will be able to start work within a year, and that it enjoys a good relationship with the company, a major developer in New York and Boston that recently started work on an apartment building nearby.

While the deal is still a work in progress, it appears the mayor's public pressure has sped up the process of luring a new partner.

"It had an impact," said John Hynes III, chief executive of Boston Global investors, a minority investor in the project that is getting bought out. The city, he said, "wanted to create a sense of urgency and impatience to get this fixed, and that message was loud and clear."

New Fed Call on Housing
By Kristina Peterson and Jeffrey Sparshott
644 words
11 February 2012
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

ORLANDO—Federal Reserve Chairman Ben Bernanke called Friday for new policies to help the nation's weak housing market, calling it a significant factor holding back the economic recovery.

"We need to continue to develop and implement policies that will help the housing sector get back on its feet," Mr. Bernanke said at a National Association of Homebuilders conference in Orlando, Fla.

The Fed chairman said the problems in the housing market—including sunken real estate values, depressed home construction and barriers to mortgage refinancing—were hurting the economy.

"The state of the housing sector has been a key impediment to a faster recovery," Mr. Bernanke said. That pressure wasn't likely to lift soon, he said, predicting that the high rate of foreclosures was "likely to continue for a while," keeping down housing prices and putting a strain on communities.

Home prices have fallen by almost a third over more than five years. Nearly 1.9 million homes have gone through into foreclosure in the past two years, and just as many are in the pipeline.

Mr. Bernanke's comments are the latest from the central bank calling for more action on housing. Last month, the Fed sent a paper to top lawmakers warning that tight mortgage-lending standards threaten to hold back the economy.

The Fed paper, and similar comments by other Fed officials, drew rebukes from some Republicans, who told the central bankers not to meddle in matters best left to Congress. On Friday, Mr. Bernanke said the paper wasn't meant to "come down with a specific set of recommendations" but rather to "put out there the main issues and arguments" and to draw attention to housing's role in the economy.

The Fed chairman's remarks Friday came a day after an announcement by government officials of a $25 billion settlement over alleged foreclosure abuses that will, in part, offer reductions in loan principal to some home owners.

Mr. Bernanke acknowledged the efforts to prevent foreclosures, but said some home owners simply wouldn't be able to afford monthly payments even with substantial loan modifications.

Instead, the Fed chairman focused on other possible tools to aid a housing recovery, including putting homes that have been foreclosed and are now creditor-owned on the market as rentals.

"With home prices falling and rents rising, it could make sense in some markets to turn some of the foreclosed homes into rental properties," he said.

Meanwhile, Mr. Bernanke said the central bank was "intensely focused" on improving credit conditions. While some tightening of credit was to be expected in the wake of the financial crisis, "the pendulum has probably swung too far in the other direction by this time," he said.

The Fed chairman faced a question from a New York real-estate investor who complained that banks won't extend more than four mortgages because of certain rules put in place by investors and banks. In 2008, Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac, the main funders of mortgages, faced soaring losses from speculators and reduced to four, from 10, the number of loans they would guarantee to any one owner.

Fannie now backs as many as 10 loans, but some banks have kept lower limits.

Those policies can be "actually pretty counterproductive," Mr. Bernanke said, suggesting they could hinder investors willing to help converting homes owned by creditors into rental properties.

"We have all these homes on the market...investors out there would like to buy them and manage them and resell them, fix them up—what's wrong with that?" he said. "We'd like to see more of that happening."

Home Prices Pop in Hot Valley Turf but Fall Elsewhere; Market in Menlo Park, Where Facebook Is Based, Surges 52.7%, yet Tech Boom Fails to Provide a Lift in Majority of Bay Area
By Jim Carlton
960 words
9 February 2012
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

The current technology boom has rippled out into the housing markets in Silicon Valley's core towns, but the ripples aren't reaching much beyond that, as home values fell at the end of last year in a majority of the Bay Area.

Median prices on existing, single-family homes rose in only 29 of 102 cities or communities around the region in the fourth quarter, compared with a year earlier, while prices declined in 72 areas, according to DataQuick, a real-estate data firm in La Jolla, Calif. Glen Ellen dropped the most, at 33.5%, while one community, Brentwood, stayed flat in the quarter.

Andrew LePage, a DataQuick analyst, cautioned that the price swings in some communities were partly due to a shifting mix of homes sold and a lower number of transactions in some cases.

Mr. LePage attributed the price declines in some towns partly to the expiration in 2010 of a federal tax credit for some home buyers. He says that credit, passed by Congress in 2008, had helped stimulate demand at the lower end of the market. Tight lending standards for jumbo mortgage loans are hurting the higher end of the market, and a tepid economic recovery outside the tech sector is dragging down both the high and low ends, he says.

All those factors could continue to hold back a full-on housing recovery, despite the pockets of high demand in Silicon Valley, Mr. LePage says. "Barring some shock to the economy, it looks like for most of the Bay Area—and California—the most you can expect we will find is a convincing rock bottom for prices in 2012," he says.

In communities in the shadow of tech giants like Facebook Inc. in Menlo Park, housing prices are on a tear. Menlo Park led the Bay Area with a 52.7% increase in its median home sales price in the fourth quarter, with prices rising to $1.29 million from $840,000 a year earlier, according to DataQuick. Palo Alto's median home-sales price jumped 12.5% to $1.1 million from $977,750, according to DataQuick.

Cities outside of Silicon Valley didn't fare as well. Sonoma, for example, experienced a 24.1% drop in the fourth quarter in its median home-sales price to $356,000 from $469,000 a year ago, while Walnut Creek prices fell 18.3% to $580,000 from $709,500, according to DataQuick.

In a positive sign for the housing market, some brokers say they are busier than they have been in years. In 2011, the Los Gatos-based Sereno Group, a real-estate brokerage, sold 1,015 homes in Silicon Valley for a total of $1.01 billion, up 16% from the 875 homes valued at $800 million it sold in 2010, says Christine Perry, a founding partner in the firm.

"It started with Palo Alto and is spilling over into other communities," Ms. Perry says.

Dick Keenly experienced that boom firsthand. The 76-year-old retired tech entrepreneur listed his three-bedroom, 2.5-bath home in Cupertino last June. A resident of Incline Village, Nev., who kept the Cupertino house as a second home, Mr. Keenly says he listed the property with Ms. Perry for $1.15 million and got full price after four offers.

"I was pleasantly surprised that I didn't have to negotiate down," says Mr. Keenly, who bought the Cupertino home in 1985 for $235,000.

In certain down markets, some buyers are jumping in because they figure prices won't go much lower. "We saw prices not really dropping and the employment market getting better," says Elizabeth Garcia, 42, who with her husband, Emilio, 49, bought a bank-owned three-bedroom, two-bath home in Gilroy in October for $264,000 to rent out. At its peak in 2006, the home was valued at $656,000, according to estimates by Zillow, the online market tracker.

"We thought jumping in now would be a good thing," says Ms. Garcia, a human-resources director. The Garcias now are planning to list their four-bedroom home in San Jose next month so they can move to a home with more acreage in Morgan Hill, says their broker, Lianne Pinkston.

Median home-sales prices dipped 0.9% year-over-year in the fourth quarter in Morgan Hill, but sales were up 5.5%, according to DataQuick. The prices in Gilroy fell 9.6% on flat sales over the same period.

Meanwhile, Andrew and Jessica Wong decided to take the plunge last summer and sold their four-bedroom home in Fremont for slightly below the asking price of $1.35 million. The couple used the money to buy a comparable-size home on a 4.5-acre lot in Morgan Hill for $1.16 million.

"It's something we wanted to do for a long time, but we put it on hold because of the market," says Ms. Wong, 42, who is self-employed. Prices in Fremont decreased 12.4% in the 2011 fourth quarter from the previous year.

With home inventories running tighter in the South Bay and many other local markets, some agents say it is a matter of time until demand exceeds supply and prices start rising across the board.

And there is another looming factor that should boost the market: Facebook, which recently filed to go public.

"Things are looking up," Ms. Pinkston says, "especially with the IPO of Facebook."

HARTFORD—A real-estate tracking group says home sales in Connecticut dropped 13% in 2011 and sank to the lowest total since the firm began monitoring property data in 1987.

The Warren Group says about 21,140 single-family homes were sold in the state last year, down from 24,270 in 2010. It was the seventh-straight year of annual sales declines.

The median price for a single-family home sold in the state last year was $243,000, a nearly 3 percent decrease compared with 2010.

Condominium sales dropped 16 percent in 2011 to about 5,700, the lowest total in more than two decades.

English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

NEW YORK—Another batch of positive economic data helped bolster the euro and growth-sensitive currencies against the dollar.

The euro briefly jumped back above $1.32 Wednesday following improving manufacturing data out of Europe and the U.S. Reports also indicated another month of steady growth in U.S. private sector payrolls and a jump in construction spending, adding to the upbeat environment.

Expectations that a Greek debt restructuring was imminent further lifted the euro, but as the day wore on and no deal was completed, the common currency gave back some of those gains.

Late Wednesday, the euro was at $1.3159 from $1.3083 late Tuesday. The euro traded at ¥100.31 from ¥99.70.

The Institute for International Finance said Wednesday that it expects a Greek deal in the "days ahead," which reduced the optimism that came earlier in the day when a Greek finance ministry official said a deal could come within hours.

The push and pull of the market on every Greek headline indicates just how on edge traders remain about the region's sovereign-debt crisis. Fitch Ratings' David Riley reiterated at a conference in New York Wednesday that a debt restructuring will be considered a default by the ratings firm.

Greece has been "a frustrating story: The [restructuring] is going to be resolved any day now, every day now, for the last two weeks," said Richard Franulovich, a senior currency strategist at Westpac Banking Corp. in New York. Assuming a deal eventually gets done and Greece "doesn't fall apart, [the euro] is probably going to retake the topside, but $1.34 is probably where it tops out." The euro hasn't traded above $1.34 since Dec. 9.

The dollar fared a bit better against the yen, which is considered even more of a safe harbor than the greenback. The dollar traded at ¥76.23 compared with ¥76.27.

Meanwhile, the pound fetched $1.5829 from $1.5761, while the dollar bought 0.9155 Swiss franc from 0.9202 franc.

The ICE Dollar Index, which tracks the U.S. dollar against a basket of currencies, was at 78.93 from 79.288 late Tuesday.

Encouraging economic reports were another primary driver of market movement throughout the session. The euro and currencies closely associated to economic growth expectations such as the Australian dollar rose against the U.S. dollar after another batch of positive economic reports.

"There is increasingly positive data out of the U.S.," said Michael Woolfolk, a senior currency strategist at Bank of New York Mellon in New York. The positive data have led to further investment in relatively higher-yielding assets and currencies at the low-yielding dollar's expense, Mr. Woolfolk said.

Private employers added 170,000 jobs in the U.S. last month, according to payroll company Automatic Data Processing Inc.'s monthly report. The Institute for Supply Management's manufacturing index hit 54.1 in January, rising from December's reading of 53.1. December U.S. construction spending tripled forecasts, rising 1.5%.

Manufacturing data out of Europe also showed that sector improved from December to January, even if not all countries saw actual expansion.

The dollar fell broadly based on the improving economic conditions. Such moves are typical because the dollar is considered a safe harbor during periods where growth expectations are weak.

When the Home Bank Closes
By Robbie Whelan

English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Keith Mullin's start-up snack-food company, Gamer Grub, needs cash to become a player.

The San Diego company, which makes bags of high-energy snack food for videogame fanatics, recently struck a promotional partnership to sell its product at stores across the U.S. as part of the launch of "Call of Duty: Modern Warfare 3," the country's top-selling videogame. While more than 3,000 stores have agreed to sell the snacks, Mr. Mullin can't get the funds he needs to hire workers and launch a marketing campaign.

In prior years, Mr. Mullin tapped his home-equity line of credit to inject cash into the business. But the credit line was canceled by his lender after the value of his home plummeted. That set off a chain of events that ended in Mr. Mullin losing his home. Now his business can't find the funds to grow.

"On one side of the coin, things are great," he says. "But on the back side of the coin, it's fire and brimstone, and it's just totally brutal."

It is well known that the housing bust has taken a devastating toll on American families, nearly three million of which have lost their homes to foreclosure. Less known is the impact that the housing collapse is having on owners of small businesses, which have often relied upon home-equity borrowing to finance the early stages of growth and development.

American business history is packed with stories of company builders, from Wal-Mart Stores Inc.'s Sam Walton to Sam Adams beer brewer Jim Koch, who got key early funding from their homes. Nearly one in three small-business owners has borrowed against the equity in his or her home, or used the home as collateral for a loan to fund the business, according to a 2009 survey conducted by Gallup for the National Federation of Independent Business.

In recent months, there have been some signs that the housing market is bottoming out. That included three straight months of gains in single-family home construction, and a report in January showing that the inventory of for-sale homes is at its lowest level since 2006. But home prices, which need to rise briskly to revive home-equity lending, fell again in November, tumbling 1.3% from the previous month, according to the S&P/Case-Shiller index released Tuesday. They are likely to fall further over the next year. The balances of home-equity lines of credit and second mortgages have tumbled 27.5% since the end of 2007.

That leaves cash-strapped small businesses in a jam. Without the ability to borrow against their home, thousands of potential entrepreneurs will sit out the recovery. Most don't have enough cash flow to get a traditional business loan from a bank. A recent study from Pepperdine University showed that 64% of start-ups were turned down by banks when seeking loans.

Mark Zandi, chief economist at Moody's Analytics, estimates that small-business owners extracted around $75 billion from their homes to fund their businesses in 2006. That has fallen to as little as $20 billion now, and isn't likely to rise much through 2012, he predicts.

Mr. Zandi took out a $40,000 business loan using his home in suburban Philadelphia as collateral in 1991 to start a predecessor company to Moody's Analytics. These days, it would be difficult for others to follow in his footsteps, he says. "That is one more reason to be nervous that small businesses won't be the source of job growth they have in past economic recoveries," he says.

The situation could get worse. Owners of start-ups are believed to have taken out risky mortgages at a higher rate than typical homeowners, putting them at a higher risk of losing their homes and their businesses over the next couple of years. Samuel Bornstein, an accounting professor at Kean University in New Jersey, and private consultant Jung Song have done research that suggests 3.7 million small businesses took out exotic loan products such as option-adjustable rate mortgages and interest-only loans. They estimate more than 500,000 are at risk of defaulting in the next few years as their monthly mortgage payments spike after these loans reset and require both principal and interest payments.

Adrian Pelkus, a San Marcos, Calif., business owner used home-equity loans to fund at least six start-up businesses in the past 15 years. He started his flagship company, A Squared Technologies Inc., which makes circuit boards for electronics manufacturers, with a $10,000 loan against his home in 1985.

But Mr. Pelkus has been thwarted by the housing bust. In 2008, he says, he hit on his best idea yet—to generate power from ocean waves. He patented the idea and produced a prototype for the product, and planned to borrow against his home to do lab tests and market the idea to investors.

But by 2009, Southern California prices had declined so far that Mr. Pelkus was underwater by more than $100,000 on the three houses he owns near San Diego. He gave up on wave power, let his patents expire and moved on to other things—developing electric cars and medical devices with his old company. He is unsure of where his next round of funding will come from, and he can't afford to hire the 12 engineers and technicians he needs for A Squared to bid on new technology projects. "Without being able to tap home equity, it's hard take an idea far enough to get traction," he says.

Many small-business owners see their home-equity lines of credit pulled or reduced when home prices fall. In its quarterly survey of about 400 small to midsize businesses, conducted this past fall, Barlow Research Associates Inc., a Minneapolis-based business data provider, found that 41% of small businesses reported seeing their home-equity credit level decrease in the previous year.

Bill Brown, a financial planner from Clarkston, Mich., had to slash his staff after his line of credit was yanked. In 2006, he borrowed $415,000 against the equity in his home, then appraised by the bank at $1.4 million. In 2009, his lender, Chase Home Mortgage, sent him a letter saying that based on the depressed value of the house, the bank would no longer honor any draws against his home-equity loan. Chase declined to comment.

Mr. Brown had to lay off seven of his 12 employees, cut back on marketing materials for his business and hold off on buying financial software. Selling the home isn't an option. Mr. Brown's daughter is disabled, and the home is outfitted to her needs. The home's value also is underwater. It is worth about $556,000, according to real estate website Zillow, and has a total of approximately $950,000 in mortgage debt on it.

Some entrepreneurs have been able to hold on to their home-equity lines by convincing banks that their businesses are safe risks.

Brad Alcock owns a small company in Missouri that imports a food packaging product from Taiwan, which he sells to suppliers who use it to seal bags of vegetables and other foods. In 2009, Bank of America revoked the line of credit he was using to fund his Taiwan purchases. He was able to convince the bank to reinstate it two weeks later.

"There's a period of time where I owe Asia, but I'm not paid yet," he says. "It had me in about a two-week panic." Bank of America confirmed that Mr. Alcock's credit line was frozen in January 2009 because of "decreasing equity in the market" but said it was reinstated a month later "based on the broad customer relationship with the bank."

Other entrepreneurs have turned to the U.S. Small Business Administration, which guarantees bank loans to entrepreneurs. For the year that ended Sept. 30, 2011, the SBA guaranteed more loans, measured by dollar amount, than in any previous year.

Mr. Mullin, the founder of Gamer Grub, drained his savings, took out $60,000 in loans backed by the SBA and borrowed from friends and family. Still, it wasn't enough to expand as rapidly as he needed to.

Last year, he began traveling and meeting with angel investors in an effort to raise $1.5 million to help the company grow. So far he has had three investor groups "kick the tires" on Gamer Grub, but had no luck in raising funds. He has been paying bills to his manufacturers using money left over from a previous business he started, which makes pet supplies.

"As an entrepreneur, it's your job to basically pull a rabbit out of your hat on a daily basis," he says. "That's how hard it is right now."

Real Estate
Home Prices Tumble
By Mia Lamar
420 words
31 January 2012
19:38
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

U.S. home prices fell again in November, according to the Standard & Poor's Case-Shiller indexes, indicating continued struggles for the beleaguered housing market.

The sector has remained sluggish despite lower prices and interest rates due to a slowly improving economy, an abundance of foreclosures and tighter mortgage requirements.

"Tighter lending standards and widespread expectations of further declines in home values have been depressing home sales on a larger scale," said economists Ellen Zentner, Aichi Amemiy and Jeffrey Greenberg of Nomura Economics Research in a note to clients. "In addition, a growing share of distressed assets in home sales that are typically sold at a 20% discount are putting downward pressure on house prices."

For November, the Case-Shiller index of 10 major metropolitan areas and the 20-city index both fell 1.3% from the previous month. David M. Blitzer, chairman of the index committee at S&P Indices, also noted that 19 of the 20 major U.S. metropolitan markets covered by the indices in November saw prices decline from October, with just Phoenix showing an increase. Atlanta, Las Vegas, Seattle and Tampa posted new index level lows.

"The only positive for the month was Phoenix, one of the hardest hit in recent years," Mr. Blitzer said. "Annual rates were little better as 18 cities and both composites were negative." Just Detroit and Washington D.C. notched year-over-year gains.

The 10-city and 20-city composites posted annual returns of negative 3.6% and negative 3.7%, respectively, compared with November 2010. Hard-hit Atlanta had the worst year-over-year performance, declining 11.8%.

On a seasonally adjusted basis, which aims to take into account the slower selling season in the winter, three cities -- Denver, Minneapolis and Phoenix -- posted monthly increases. The overall 20-city index was down 0.7% from the previous month on a seasonally adjusted basis.

There have been indications more recently of some stabilization in home sales, and economists say that may show up in price data later this year. "Economic growth has accelerated, jobs growth has picked-up and confidence has increased. Banks even seem a little more willing to lend. After the normal lead time of around six months, the resulting rise in home sales should go some way to bringing an end to the five-year-long decline in house prices," said Paul Dales, senior U.S. economist at Capital Economics.

Phil Izzo contributed to this article.

Heard on the Street
Markets
Housing's Firmer Foundation
By Justin Lahart
315 words
1 February 2012
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Tuesday's report that prices continue to slump has tempered hopes that the U.S. housing market is finally turning. But right now, the connection between what the S&P/Case-Shiller index says and what is actually going on with housing may be lukewarm at best.

The 20-city home-price index slipped 1.3% in November from October, putting it 3.7% below its year-earlier level. The implication: Supply has continued to outstrip demand, pushing prices lower, despite the upbeat tempo of other housing data and positive vibes from home builders.

The Case-Shiller index is closely watched for a reason. It was quicker than a U.S. government price index to show just how bad things were as housing came off the rails in 2007. The difference: The Federal Housing Finance Agency index includes only homes with mortgages guaranteed by Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac, while the Case-Shiller index includes those backed by jumbo and subprime mortgages.

Many homes that were backed by subprime mortgages are now being sold in foreclosure. They aren't in nearly as good condition as when they were last bought, and are selling for less than if they had been properly maintained. Because the Case-Shiller index is based on repeat sales, such homes may be biasing it downward.

Moreover, the Case-Shiller index is based on a three-month average of sales, so its November level includes transactions that were completed in October and September. Consider that it takes about two months between a sale and a closing (often longer with mortgage hassles these days), and you are talking about deals agreed on in the summer, when recession fears filled the air. Things now look better. Home prices probably do, too.

Property: New Construction Slid 31% in 2011
By Joseph De Avila
528 words
31 January 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
A20
English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

New York City's construction industry was dealt another tough year as the value of construction projects that began in 2011 sank 31% compared with 2010, new data released on Monday said.

Reduced government spending on infrastructure and very few new, large commercial buildings contributed to the building industry's poor 2011. The value of construction starts fell by $6.2 billion in 2011 to $13.8 billion, according to the analysis by the New York Building Congress, an association representing real-estate developers and construction companies.

The lone bright spot was that residential construction projects rose nearly 25% to $2.9 million in 2011, the report said.

Last year, two construction projects costing $500 million or more were started in the city. In 2010, there were seven, including three -- at the World Trade Center site, the renovation of Madison Square Garden and the groundbreaking at the Barclays Arena -- that accounted for $6 billion of new construction.

Richard T. Anderson, president of the New York Building Congress, cautions that just a few projects can skew annual figures. But "there really is a slowdown on the nonresidential side," he said.

The value of nonresidential development, which includes offices, hotels and schools, fell by $5.3 billion, or 39%. Government infrastructure projects also were off for the third straight year, falling last year by $1.4 billion, or 35%.

The construction industry lost 4,500 jobs in New York City, which was the most of any industry in 2011, according to real-estate services firm Eastern Consolidated. Since September 2009, the construction industry has lost 14,000 jobs, or nearly 12% of its work force.

Pent-up demand for rental units and lenders who have become more willing to finance construction for rental apartments likely contributed to the improvement in residential construction starts last year, said Melissa Pianko, executive vice president of development, with Gotham Organization, a real-estate developer.

"Strong developers are accessing the debt markets in ways they didn't in the past," she said.

Gotham is building a 1,238-unit rental-apartment tower on West 45th Street called Gotham West. It was the most expensive residential project to break ground at the city in 2011, with construction costs estimated to be just over $360 million.

Financing for luxury condo development also remains relatively robust, said Vishaan Chakrabarti, the director of the Center for Urban Real Estate at Columbia University.

"The luxury market in condos is still quite strong, but that middle market still has a ways to go," Mr. Chakrabarti said.

Strict financing standards are one of the main reasons New York City isn't churning out apartments as it did in years past. Last year, 10,000 residential units received permission to begin construction in the city, up from 6,000 in 2009, Mr. Anderson said.

But 10,000 new housing units a year is only half the amount the city needs to meet new demand. "It's encouraging that the trend is upward but it needs to get a lot better than that," he said.

U.S. News: In Florida, Politics Begins at Homes --- Disillusioned Voters Demand Remedies to Mortgage Problems as Republican Candidates Get Set for Primary
By Arian Campo-Flores
1714 words
31 January 2012
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
7
English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

DELTONA, Fla. -- Any politician who wants to use Florida's housing collapse as a way to win the vote of Claudio Fernandez faces a stiff challenge.

Mr. Fernandez thought he had secured a piece of the American dream for his two kids when he bought a pair of homes here in 2006 that he planned to rent out and eventually pass on to them. Then the housing market tanked, some of his renters lost their jobs and he had to tap his savings to cover his losses.

After two years of maddening negotiations with lenders, Mr. Fernandez says, he is about to lose one home in a short sale -- in which the price is less than the money owed on the property -- and is still trying to modify the loan on the second. In the process, he says he has lost nearly $50,000, taken on more debt and trashed his credit rating.

Though he mainly blames the banks and himself, he fumes at political leaders, too. "They bailed out business and Wall Street" but "failed to bail out the American citizen," says Mr. Fernandez, a 43-year-old Republican who voted for President Barack Obama in 2008. Looking to November, he says, "if it's the current field -- Obama and these clowns in the Republican party -- I'd rather not vote." He has no plans to cast a ballot in Florida's Republican primary on Tuesday.

Many share Mr. Fernandez's frustration as well as his political reaction in foreclosure-racked Volusia County, a key electoral battleground at the eastern end of the Interstate 4 corridor in Central Florida, where elections in the state are often decided. Those who have been touched directly by foreclosure -- 28% of Volusia's mortgages were in foreclosure or seriously delinquent as of October -- along with neighbors who have watched their property values plummet are angry and restive.

It makes for a volatile brew as Florida Republicans prepare to vote in their primary Tuesday. Over the weekend, the top Republican contenders again dug into each other as polls showing big leads for Mitt Romney put rival Newt Gingrich on the defensive.

In Naples, Fla., on Sunday, Mr. Romney tried to link Mr. Gingrich's consulting work for mortgage giant Freddie Mac to Florida's troubled housing market. "I know how bad housing is here. . ..You know how much home values have come down,"" he said, blaming lending practices by Freddie and Fannie Mae for the downturn. Mr. Gingrich, he said, was paid "to stand up and say these programs should continue the way they are; these institutions are fine. . ..The people of Florida have had enough of Freddie Mac and Fannie Mae.""

Mr. Gingrich, the former House speaker, said Mr. Romney had built his lead in the polls through negative campaigning. "He has a basic policy of carpet bombing his opponent," Mr. Gingrich said on "Fox News Sunday."

Rather than heed the calls of candidates who blame one another for the mess, many voters are merely disillusioned, saying they hear nothing from any of the candidates suggesting they offer a path out. Residents affected by the housing slump often say the upshot is they can't decide who to support or may choose not to vote at all.

Volusia is one of three swing counties in swing states -- Florida, Ohio and Colorado -- that The Wall Street Journal is visiting periodically this year to gauge how the election campaign is unfolding. As the Republican presidential candidates tour Florida, foreclosures have been an especially hot topic.

Though last year Mr. Romney said the foreclosure process should be allowed to "run its course," he has moderated his tone in Florida, saying he is open to working with lenders to stabilize the housing market. Mr. Gingrich has said he would encourage banks to approve more short sales of properties whose owners owe more than the homes are worth and attacked Mr. Romney for his own investments in bank- and mortgage-company stocks and bonds.

In his State of the Union address last week Mr. Obama proposed legislation that would help homeowners refinance mortgages at lower interest rates. The administration says it has already helped millions of families with existing programs, including one that has led to nearly 910,000 permanent loan modifications.

Few say they have noticed such efforts in Volusia, where "For Sale" signs cover neighborhoods and residents are grappling with 10.1% unemployment.

Though Florida accounted for less than 8% of all mortgages in the U.S. in September, it made up nearly 25% of all loans in foreclosure, according to the Mortgage Bankers Association. In Volusia, almost a third of mortgages were in foreclosure or seriously delinquent in October, according to CoreLogic Inc., a research firm. The median price of a single-family home tumbled to $98,800 in October, down 54% from $217,000 in 2006, says Morgan Gilreath, the county property appraiser.

Nationally, five states -- Florida, Nevada, Arizona, Michigan and California -- have borne the brunt of the housing collapse. All of them, except California, are expected to be important presidential-campaign battlegrounds. The two other swing counties the Journal is visiting this year have fared better. In Ohio's Hamilton County, about 10% of borrowers were in foreclosure or seriously delinquent in October, and in Colorado's Arapahoe County, about 7% were.

In Volusia, the city of Deltona -- the county's biggest, with 85,000 people -- was hard-hit by the mortgage meltdown. A bedroom community of Orlando with no downtown and no hospital, Deltona experienced a frenzy of building during the housing boom, followed by a collapse.

One recent day, real-estate broker Sheree Frazier pulled up a multiple-listing-service map of the area, including the neighboring cities of DeBary and DeLand, on her computer. Apart from a scattering of symbols showing pending or completed sales, it was blanketed with gavels, signifying foreclosures. "This is far from being over," she said.

Ms. Frazier, who sold her personal home in a short sale, says there is only one solution: Banks need to write down the principal on homeowners' mortgages and take the losses. "We need [a president] who's willing to take the bull by the horns, and I mean the banks," says Ms. Frazier, a Republican. Mr. Obama "ain't done squat." Yet none of the Republican candidates embrace the dramatic step she proposes to end the foreclosure wave, and she doesn't like any of them either, she says.

People interviewed for this article who have faced, or gone through, foreclosure uniformly say the experience so consumed and upended their lives that they had little time to deal with anything else.

Mercedes Coviella, a 40-year-old clerical temp who is still going through the process, says she and her husband separated because of the stress, leaving her a single mother of two. "I'm so busy with my day-to-day duties that I haven't had a chance to watch the [GOP] debates," says Ms. Coviella, a Republican from DeBary. "I honestly don't have any clue what anyone's talking about now."

Such upheaval makes it hard to gauge the political effect of the housing crisis. Those ensnared by it share an antipathy toward the banks. And they say they fault both parties for failing to resolve the mess and favoring the interests of Wall Street over those of homeowners.

"I blame the Republican administration at the time" the housing downturn began, says Mr. Fernandez. "And then I blame the Democratic administration for the handling of it."

None of those interviewed say they have heard anything close to a solution from the current crop of candidates. "Most of the candidates come from wealthy backgrounds," says Ms. Coviella. "It's hard for them to see people on my level worried about paying groceries."

Among partisan voters, the housing crisis seems to have reaffirmed pre-existing views. One 68-year-old Republican man from DeLand who lost his home to a short sale says the government should get out of the way. "You have to be self-reliant," he says, adding that he plans to vote for the eventual GOP nominee.

On the other hand, Lee Banks, a 54-year-old Democrat from DeLand who has been trying to negotiate a loan modification for more than two years, says government should do more. "A man in need should be able to get some kind of help," he says. He plans to vote for Mr. Obama.

Voters in the middle, though, say they will have a harder time choosing. "I'll vote for whoever gives my [unemployed] husband a job," says Judy Evans, a 59-year-old DeLand resident who declined to give her party affiliation. But she says politicians are "all full of bull. They get in and don't do anything."

Some say they are so disgusted at the government's inability to fix the economy that they plan to sit out this election. "I'm not voting this year," says a 66-year-old Democrat from DeLand who voted for President Obama in 2008 and has since sold her home in a short sale.

Those who have experienced foreclosure directly aren't the only ones battered by housing's collapse. Deltona "has taken a huge fiscal hit" because of declining property-tax revenue, says Democratic City Commissioner Heidi Herzberg. That forced the city to cut jobs and pare expenses. As residents moved out and job losses accelerated, local businesses shut down and some shopping plazas became wastelands. Foreclosed homes became neighborhood eyesores,. Though the local economy is perking up, it has a long way to go.

Chris Schmidt, a 39-year-old cable salesman from Deltona, says at least five of the houses on his block have gone through foreclosure, including the ones on either side of his. "My property value has plummeted," he says. Like the 46% of mortgage borrowers in Volusia County who owe more than their homes are worth, according to CoreLogic, his home loan is underwater.

Nick Timiraos contributed to this article.

None of those interviewed say they have heard anything close to a solution from the current crop of candidates. "Most of the candidates come from wealthy backgrounds," says Ms. Coviella. "It's hard for them to see people on my level worried about paying groceries."

Among deeply partisan voters, the housing crisis seems to have reaffirmed their pre-existing views. One 68-year-old Republican man from DeLand who lost his home to a short sale says the government should simply get out of the way. "You have to be self-reliant," he says, adding that he plans to vote for the eventual GOP nominee.

On the other hand, Lee Banks, a 54-year-old Democrat from DeLand who has been trying to negotiate a loan modification for more than two years, says government should do more. "A man in need should be able to get some kind of help," he says. He plans to vote for Mr. Obama, who he says would have done more for homeowners had he not been blocked by Republicans in Congress.

Voters in the middle, though, say they will have a harder time choosing. "I'll vote for whoever gives my [unemployed] husband a job," says Judy Evans, a 59-year-old DeLand resident who declined to give her party affiliation. But she says politicians are "all full of bull. They get in and don't do anything."

Some say they are so disgusted at the government's inability to fix the economy that they plan to sit out this election. "I'm not voting this year," says a 66-year-old Democrat from DeLand who voted for President Obama in 2008 and has since sold her home in a short sale. "I'm not convinced that any of [the candidates] are for the people."

Those who have experienced foreclosure directly aren't the only ones battered by the housing market's collapse. Deltona "has taken a huge fiscal hit" because of declining property-tax revenue, says Democratic city commissioner Heidi Herzberg. That forced the city to cut jobs and pare back expenses on everything from new vehicles to office water coolers.

As residents moved out and job losses accelerated, local businesses shut down and some shopping plazas became wastelands. Foreclosed homes became neighborhood eyesores, with overgrown yards and deteriorating facades. Though the local economy is perking up a bit, it has a long way to go.

Chris Schmidt, a 39-year-old cable salesman from Deltona, says at least five of the houses on his block have gone through foreclosure, including the ones on either side of his. "My property value has plummeted," he says. Yet he is trapped. "I would like to upgrade," he says, "but without being able to sell my house, that's very hard to do."

Like the 46% of mortgage borrowers in Volusia County who owe more than their homes are worth, according to CoreLogic, his home loan is underwater.

That has Mr. Schmidt, a Republican, in a sour mood as the election season gathers steam. "I'm really upset with the bailouts," he says. "I was one of those people who worked very hard to make sure I avoided foreclosure…. It seems like they rewarded the people who created the problem."

Mr. Schmidt says he is open to voting for a candidate from either party. But so far, none of them have convinced him they can turn things around.

Nick Timiraos contributed to this article.

Personal Finance
Housing Remains a Buyer's Market
By Amy Hoak
850 words
29 January 2012
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ2
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

A majority of Americans recently surveyed say now is a good time to buy a home. That's no surprise, given that record-low mortgage interest rates and bargain home prices are boosting affordability.

But selling a home? That's a different story.

According to 71% of the 1,000 people surveyed by Fannie Mae in December, now is a good time to buy a house. But only 11% think it's a good time to sell.

That's because sellers sense that even if the housing market and the economy continue to show signs of improvement in 2012, the good news likely won't be good enough for buyers to return to the market in droves—even if they can buy a home for a steal.

"For people to start buying in larger volume, they need to see home prices go up a bit," says Ingo Winzer, president of Local Market Monitor, a firm that analyzes housing markets for bankers.

Many potential buyers also are waiting to see the jobs picture improve, which will give them confidence in the stability of their own employment, Mr. Winzer says.

Improvements Ahead

Still, various forecasts and surveys suggest better times for the housing market this year:

Sales of existing homes are expected to grow between 2% and 5% in 2012, according a recent forecast from Freddie Mac.

A recent survey of about 1,000 Re/Max real-estate agents found that 39% of agents think prices have hit bottom in their market, while almost 75% think home prices in their markets will have stopped declining by the end of 2012.

The number of improving housing markets rose to 76 in January, from 41 in December, according to the Improving Markets Index, from First American Financial Corp. and the National Association of Home Builders.

Nationwide, home prices are expected to be relatively flat in 2012, says Alex Villacorta, director of research and analytics at Clear Capital, a provider of real-estate asset-valuation data for financial-services companies. Indeed, 2012 seems to be a turning point before a healthier and sustained recovery in 2013, he says.

If You Can Hold Out

While now still may not be the perfect time to sell a home, it may be time for home sellers to get their places ready for a sale next year.

Of course, markets vary. Prices already are on the rise in some places, including parts of Florida, Washington, D.C., and Dayton, Ohio, Mr. Villacorta says.

But other markets—including Chicago, Atlanta, Detroit and Las Vegas—continue to be on a "downward slide," according to a December report from Realtor.com.

Either way, holding out until next year could mean a quicker and more profitable sale.

"From a seller's point of view, it's still a little early, though tempting, to put the house up for sale and expect a lot of demand," Mr. Villacorta says. "Unless there are circumstances that dictate they have to sell now, certainly waiting and tracking the markets a little bit more would be a more prudent thing to do."

Still, some sellers have delayed their moving decisions for years now. For those champing at the bit to make a sale and move on with their lives, 2012 may offer glimmers of hope.

"There are a lot of people over the last few years that decided to put their life on hold," says Budge Huskey, president of real-estate brokerage Coldwell Banker. Some now are saying, 'I've waited long enough. I can't put life on hold forever,' " Mr. Huskey says.

The market is finally nearing the point where people who don't need to sell for financial reasons are starting to consider a move for lifestyle-related reasons, he says, such as a growing family that would be more comfortable in a larger home.

The good news for them: Inventory plunged to a 6.2-month supply in December, from a 12.4 month supply in July 2010, according to the National Association of Realtors. That means there are fewer sellers competing for buyers. (The month-supply figure is how long it would take to sell all the homes on the market now based on the current rate of sales per month. The higher the number, the more sellers there are looking for buyers.)

If You Can't Wait

If you plan to sell a home this year, get the house in the best possible condition and price it to sell before it hits the market, Mr. Huskey says.

An appealing online listing, complete with quality photographs, is also crucial to bring traffic to your home.

"The buyer has the opportunity to prescreen all the homes online and see only the few that really shine online," Mr. Huskey says, so a seller should do everything he or she can to get on a buyer's shortlist of homes to physically visit.

Amy Hoak is a reporter for MarketWatch. Read more at marketwatch.com[http://www.marketwatch.com/].

U.S. News: Businesses Ramp Up Spending on Goods --- Manufacturers Propel 3% Monthly Rise in Orders, but New-Home Sales Reflect Real Estate's Long Road to Recovery
By Josh Mitchell and Robbie Whelan
783 words
27 January 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
A3
English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

American businesses stepped up their spending going into the new year, propelled by an economic upswing that has yet to lift much of the housing market.

New orders for U.S. durable goods -- those lasting longer than three years, such as automobiles and kitchen appliances -- rose 3% in December from November, the Commerce Department said Thursday. The data suggest that business spending on equipment climbed for the first time in three months, indicating renewed confidence among companies.

However, a separate report Thursday reflected weakness in housing -- a departure from a recent stream of signals that the sector may be inching out of its recession-era depths. Sales of newly built homes were weaker than expected in December, falling 2.2% from November to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 307,000 homes.

For all of 2011, builders sold 302,000 new homes, which is the smallest number since the Census Bureau began tracking such sales in 1963.

A report last week said sales of existing homes -- which usually fetch lower prices than new ones, particularly amid a glut of foreclosed properties -- rose in December for the third straight month, bringing the supply of homes listed for sale to the lowest level since 2006.

David Crowe, chief economist for the National Association of Home Builders, expects new-home sales to rise to 360,000 this year, an improvement from 2011 but still about one-third of the rate of sales during the peak years of the past decade.

"It's been a terrible year," said Craig Perry, chief executive of Fort Lauderdale, Fla.-based Centerline Homes, which sold 300 homes last year, down from 400 in 2010. "It's an inventory thing. You have to wait for the inventory to clear. There's a lot of resale that's distressed out there, a lot that's not distressed, and then there's the shadow market. We just need to reach an equilibrium."

While the housing market has much ground to regain, corporations are poised to boost production in coming months, powering the U.S. recovery as other parts of the world slow down.

Several factors are at play. Demand for automobiles has taken off as Americans who put off purchases during the recession and early in the recovery are now replacing cars and buying new ones.

In addition, manufacturers -- who saw business slow over the summer -- say demand is picking up.

"When you put it all together it looks like we have some good momentum in manufacturing," said Jonathan Basile, an economist at Credit Suisse. "It's been a strong point in the recovery, and it looks like it remains a strong point because it's still growing."

Thursday's durable-goods numbers, along with indicators of a brighter outlook among employers, signal pockets of strength in the American economy even as Asia loses momentum and the euro zone teeters toward recession.

The increase in durable goods was spurred by demand for airplanes. However, there were gains in every major category, from primary metals to machinery, with the exceptions being electrical equipment and defense products. Orders for nondefense capital goods excluding aircraft -- a proxy for business spending -- rose 2.9%, after two months of declines.

Caterpillar Inc., for one, on Thursday reported a 60% rise in fourth-quarter profit and said it expects record sales in 2012. The Peoria, Ill., maker of bulldozers and other earth-moving machinery said it is more optimistic now than three months ago that a global recession won't occur in 2012.

"The improvement in orders has created growing backlogs as order books are swelling" at U.S. manufacturers, said economist Joel Naroff of Naroff Economic Advisors in Holland, Pa. "That is occurring despite firms accelerating their shipping activity."

Another report Thursday reflected growing confidence. The Conference Board said its index of leading economic indicators, designed to predict the direction of the economy, rose four-tenths of a point in December to 94.3. The board said the rise was in part due to a change in the way the index is calculated.

Separately, the number of Americans filing initial claims for jobless benefits rose by 21,000 last week to a seasonally adjusted 377,000, the Labor Department said Thursday.

That followed a big drop in claims the previous week. The four-week moving average, which smooths out weekly volatility in the numbers, fell by 2,500 to 377,500.

The Property Report
Deal of the Week: From Builder to Land Baron
By Robbie Whelan
757 words
25 January 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C6
English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

On Florida's sun-drenched Gulf Coast, the sale of about 320 home sites in Fiddlers Creek, a huge, luxury-home community near Naples, speaks volumes about the changing economics of home building.

Developer Aubrey Ferrao sold 227 lots to Lennar Corp. and 91 lots to D.R. Horton Inc. for an average of about $100,000 each, a surprisingly high number in a state where home sale prices continue to languish.

Housing experts say Mr. Ferrao was able to charge that much because public home builders these days prefer to buy relatively smaller parcels of land in markets that already have seen sales instead of bigger, riskier tracts. Fiddlers Creek, which already has amenities like a golf course, has sold 1,743 homes, with another 4,257 planned.

In some parts of Florida like Naples and nearby Marco Island, lot prices are at the highest levels since the boom, says Brad Hunter, chief economist at Metrostudy, a housing-research firm that tracks land sales. "Builders are willing to pay the premium not to have a lot of land on their books," he says. "Some people wonder if they're making any money at all."

Indeed, the story of the Fiddlers Creek deal also shows how lousy the home-sales business has become. Mr. Ferrao, who purchased land for Fiddlers Creek starting in 1993, spent the boom years building homes and selling them for an average of $1.3 million apiece to Midwestern retirees and vacationers. He says his profit margin was 40%, thanks to the soaring housing market and his low land cost.

Lennar and D.R. Horton plan to sell homes for $380,000 to $750,000, company officials say. They decline to discuss profit margin.

"People can now buy a Bentley at Buick prices," Mr. Ferrao says.

A native of the South Indian coastal region of Goa, Mr. Ferrao has seen both the ups and downs of the housing cycle. A U.S. citizen since the mid-1980s, he moved to Naples after visiting it for a wedding, and the area, with its warm climate and "tropical pace of life," reminded him of his home. "I said to myself, 'I've got to find some way to get down here,'" he recalls.

Around 1986, Mr. Ferrao began buying coastal land and building high-rise condominiums. In 1993, he ventured into the world of single-family homes, borrowing $150 million from a Japanese lender to buy 1,300 acres of foreclosed land and calling the project Fiddlers Creek. Over the next decade, he added land until the community was 4,000 acres and borrowed more to build amenities.

For years, he was one of the region's biggest builder-developers, selling Italian-style villas with high-end kitchens and polished-brass doorknobs, near a well-groomed golf course and not far from the Gulf beaches.

But the housing crash hit Naples hard: Home values, once red-hot, withered. The median price of a home in Florida fell to $135,000 by the end of 2011, down 45% from its high of $247,000 in the peak year of 2006, based on the Florida Association of Realtors.

In Naples, the declines have been even more severe. In 2006, homes were selling at a median price of $530,000 in the upscale Naples-Marco Island market; in Naples, they had fallen to $210,000 by the end of 2011.

Fiddlers Creek also used municipal bonds to finance its infrastructure, and Mr. Ferrao continues to battle with the project's bondholders over who should pay debt service on some $100 million of the bonds, and when they should be paid. In early 2010, with no apparent end to the housing crisis, Fiddler's Creek filed for bankruptcy protection, listing a $165 million mortgage in addition to the bond debt. Gulf Bay Group of Cos., Mr. Ferrao's company, said the project had lost more than half of its $700 million value.

Mr. Ferrao restructured his debts and retained control of the project after it emerged from bankruptcy court in September, but his days of selling multimillion-dollar homes in gated communities in the Naples area were over. He stopped building at Fiddler's Creek, and instead began to sell the land he had accumulated there to large national builders.

"I can make more money in Naples selling land than I can selling homes," he says.

U.S. News: Sales Stir Hope for Housing Market
By Robbie Whelan
640 words
21 January 2012
The Wall Street Journal

English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Sales of previously owned homes rose in December for the third straight month, bringing the supply of homes listed for sale to the lowest level since 2006 and offering a glimmer of hope that the housing market could be starting to climb out of a profound downturn.

Existing-home sales increased 5% in December from a month earlier, to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 4.61 million units, the National Association of Realtors said Friday. Lawrence Yun, the Realtors' chief economist, called the December gain "a good finish to a very tough year."

Many economists had predicted that 2011 would be the worst year on record for existing home sales, but the year ended with 4.26 million sales, about 1.6% higher than the 4.19 million existing homes sold in 2010. Market-watchers attributed this to a minor surge in sales at year-end, driven by historically low mortgage rates, falling prices, active investor-buyers and increasing consumer confidence.

Still, economists cautioned that it's too early to assume that the market is recovering. "These were positive numbers, but that doesn't mean the market is getting better. Lenders have been trying to get rid of distressed homes, and investors been snapping them up," said Patrick Newport, chief economist at IHS Global Insight. According to the Realtors report, investors purchased 21% of all homes in December, up from 19% in November.

The inventory of homes for sale declined in December to 2.38 million, the equivalent of a 6.2-month supply, assuming the pace of sales remain at December's level. A six-month supply of homes typically is considered healthy, although NAR's numbers don't take into account the "shadow inventory" of homes that are either in foreclosure or on bank balance sheets and not yet listed for sale.

Prices, meanwhile, continue to fall. The median price in December was $164,500, down 2.5% from a year earlier. Prices were down in all regions except the West, where prices rose slightly, compared with a year ago. For all of 2011, the median was $166,100, the lowest since 2002.

"What you really want to see is sales going up, inventories going down, and prices going up, not down," said David Semmens, an economist with Standard Chartered. "People still feel they can hold off buying a house because the recovery won't be that aggressive. It's still very much a buyer's market."

That buyer's market allowed Andrew Gonzales, a 24-year-old police officer in Santa Fe, N.M., to be picky about price when looking for a home for himself and his three-year-old daughter. He closed last month on a $132,000, three-bedroom home in Rio Rancho, a suburb of Albuquerque, after the price was cut twice. Just before closing, the home was appraised for $18,000 higher than the sales price, at $150,000, by a private appraiser.

"I got tired of paying rent, and I'm a single father, so I wanted a home for my daughter," he said. "I was just waiting for the price to come down."

Vision Equity, a company that buys foreclosed homes at auctions in Indianapolis, stepped up the volume of its purchases this winter, buying about 45 homes a month in October, November and December, compared with about 30 homes a month last summer.

"There's a lot of cash investor activity right now," said Steve Olson, a spokesman for Vision Equity. "The chatter at the courthouse was, there's going to be a lot more product coming on the market, and the pricing is going to be good for investors. And we prepared our own investors for that."

The Wall Street Journal Europe

English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The U.S. consumer is starting off 2012 on a somewhat sounder footing than before, as everyday prices ease and the labor market strengthens.

The consumer-price index, which measures what Americans pay for everything from automobiles to groceries, was unchanged in December from the previous month, the Labor Department said Thursday. Overall inflation was 3% for the year -- the greatest increase since 2007 -- but prices have been flat or down since October, largely because of a drop in gasoline costs.

Meanwhile, the number of Americans filing initial jobless claims tumbled last week to a nearly four-year low, falling 50,000 to a seasonally adjusted 352,000, the Labor Department said. That shows that employers are continuing to slow the pace of layoffs, which will likely translate into more jobs following a recent pickup in hiring.

To be sure, Americans still are feeling an economic strain. More than 13 million individuals are out of work -- and millions more are stuck in part-time jobs -- and the unemployment rate remains high at 8.5%. In 2011, wages didn't keep up with inflation, prompting many consumers to dip into savings and, more recently, to step up borrowing.

The housing market, which has weighed on the recovery, has much ground to regain. A Commerce Department report Thursday showed that home construction fell 4.1% in December, capping what one economist called one of the "worst years on record" for the home-construction industry.

Analysts also cautioned that an economic shock -- such as last year's gas-price spike and the earthquake in Japan -- could knock U.S. consumers and the recovery off course.

But for now, some Americans are finding they have a hint more breathing room than they did just a few months ago.

Christelle Durand, of Queens, N.Y., spent last summer and fall unemployed while she looked for a job in sports management, after earning her master's degree from Georgetown University.

After dozens of job applications went unanswered, Ms. Durand, who is 23 years old, signed up with a temporary-help agency and two months ago started as a support staffer for a design team in the fashion industry.

She said she feels more secure now that she has a steady income and can start paying down her $8,000 student loan. She enjoys the occasional dinner out and is focused on turning her temporary position into a permanent one. "I'm comfortable here, I'm comfortable doing what I'm doing," Ms. Duran said.

If more Americans get jobs and become more confident about the economy, they will be more likely to spend money, said Stephen Stanley, an economist at Pierpont Securities LLC. An increase in consumer spending -- which represents about two-thirds of the economy and has been a driver of the recovery -- would help the economy grow in coming months.

Increased consumer confidence, prompted by jobs gains, lower costs and higher wages, could help end that standoff, Mr. Stanley said.

Thursday's consumer-price report showed that not all costs are going down. Energy prices fell 1.3% on a 2% drop in gasoline. But food prices rose 0.2% amid higher costs for cereals and meats. "Core" prices -- which strip out volatile food and energy -- edged 0.1% higher from November to December. That was due largely to rental increases that sent overall housing costs up .1%.
